030726999X|4.0|Short Review: if you've already read the first two books of this series, the third is a must-buy. Simple as that.<br /><br />Detailed Review: The story kicks off right where the second book left us. Lisbeth Salander has been very badly wounded and is immediately taken to a hospital for emergency surgery. Her half-brother, the monster of a man named Ronald Niedermann, has escaped and is on the run after murdering a police officer.<br /><br />Our male hero and often-times womanizer Mikael Blomkvist has uncovered the secret about Alexander Zalachenko, Salander's father, and desperately works against time to figure out the incredible story of corruption that resulted in Salander being locked up in a psychiatric ward as a child. The story will leave you shocked at times, as the (numerous) criminals work behind the scenes using any means necessary to ensure Blomkvist and the police don't uncover their secrets. There are twists and turns at every stage, and even while being cooped up in the hospital for over half the book, Salander plays her part in helping Blomkvist as well as assisting Erika Berger in an interesting subplot. And just like the second book, there will be a few times where you go \\"did that seriously just happen??\\" There are a LOT of characters to keep track of, but they all serve their purpose well.<br /><br />Inevitably, the book moves on to the trial and the charges brought against Salander. She and her allies, along with lawyer Annika Giannini (Mikael's sister), must work to overturn the seemingly incontrovertible evidence the prosecution believes it has. Meanwhile, Blomkvist continues working at a restless pace to discover just who the behind-the-scenes criminals are as well as putting together a book detailing Salander's life story.<br /><br />There are a few flaws, however. I do not know how Swedish courtrooms work in real life, but the trial seemed to have a very, very relaxed atmosphere. An example: Giannini will be questioning a witness for a little while, and then will turn around and have a conversation with Salander, out loud in front of everyone, uninterrupted. Prosecutor Ekstrom barely says anything aside from the first day of the trial. Holger Palmgren gives \\"advice\\" to the judge on one occasion. It's all a bit too relaxed.<br /><br />Also, while in the hospital, Salander and Zalachenko are placed only two rooms apart from each other. Here are two family members who tried to kill each other, and yet only two sets of walls separate them. Surely the doctors are smarter than that.<br /><br />Overall, the conclusion to this wonderful series will not disappoint, and it is only a shame that Larsson is no longer with us, as there was a fourth book (and possibly more) in the works. Alas, it was not to be, and Larsson does a wonderful job tying up the loose ends at different stages throughout the book.<br /><br />Finally, some fun discussion questions for folks who have already finished the book (SPOILERS INCLUDED):<br /><br />1) What was the point of including Camilla Salander as a character since we never get to know her? Was she perhaps meant to be revealed in the unfinished fourth book?<br /><br />2) Did anyone else think the conclusion to Erika Berger's subplot (Fredriksson's reason for harassing her) was absurd? The plot was interesting, but the conclusion was disappointing.<br /><br />3) Do you think Blomkvist and Figuerola will live happily ever after or can he not keep his hands off of Berger?<br /><br />4) Is Salander truly capable of living a full, happy life? Or will she always be running around, unable to strike a deep human connection with anyone else?ave a conversation with Salander, out loud in front of everyone, uninterrupted. She made my laugh, she was strong, and she loves unconditionally, even when she has to make hard choices between friends.<br /><br />Leo definitely amazed me.  He possesses great skill, is sensitive to those around him, and has a past he wants to bury.  He was a crack-up and had the funniest one-liners of the group. I want more of all these fantastic characters!<br /><br />STORY NOTES -<br />This is a crazy story, in all the best ways.  Nonstop action, intense physical and emotional struggles, and a lovely new budding romance.  Then there's the mystery of the new villains, and how to defeat them.  Who's behind it all?!  I also really like how the POV changes added to the atmosphere, giving a different perspective to everything and everyone.<br /><br />There are quite a bit more visions, dreams, and prophecies in this book, which I really enjoyed.  new gods are introduced, some good, some funny, some evil.  I loved the Roman aspect of the story (brings up a lot of questions); Roman/Gladiator training = definitely amazing.  It's a great way to learn mythology, even the lesser known stories.  Let's just say - I love Heroes.  Wanna know who my favorite is?  Duh!  Percy! :D<br /><br />SUMMING IT UP -<br />Lightning.  It's fast, bright, and awe-inspiring. 0307888908|5.0|Reid Hoffman, who is less well known than other Silicon Valley icons like Zuckerberg or Jobs, has been called \\"*the* role model for entrepreneurs\\" by tech publications, and for very good reason. He's a hugely successful investor who not only has had a hand in some of the most successful tech companies around today (including Zuckerberg's Facebook) but is also a hugely successful entrepreneur and founder - an incredibly rare achievement in today's tech world. He's currently chairman and co-founder of the professional networking site LinkedIn and was an executive at PayPal.  Ben Casnocha is an intensely curious young author. Anyone familiar with his extensive online writing and earlier book, My Start-Up Life, knows the quality of insight he provides. That voice certainly shines through in this book. Not only is he a true intellectual, he also has the business experience as a founder of his own software company to be able to write authoritatively on a topic like this.  Speaking of that topic, careers and career development is one of the most broad topics one can imagine, and considering the breadth of topics and points these two cover, the book is remarkably coherent and engaging. References to LinkedIn and how to use it effectively are tactfully sprinkled throughout the book and do not dominate the bulk of the discussion.  Two of the key threads:  - Relationships are the most important aspect of one's career.  The authors use a brilliant analogy for thinking about one's relationships: I-to-the-We. Your individual strengths matter, but when surrounded by the right individuals, your ability to effect change is raised exponentially by the power of your network.   A refreshing change from typical business books, the authors show how to build genuine relationships that benefit both individuals. They stress helping first and not keeping score.  This idea is epitomized by the little-known story that Hoffman actually introduced Mark Zuckerberg to Facebook's first outside investor and himself (Hoffman) invested in the very first round of financing. Facebook, for now, has the greater market cap and the larger member base. Their respective companies are often pitted against each other in the media, and Hoffman's willingness to help a young Zuckerberg building a competing social network shows Hoffman's commitment to follow his own advice.  - Have a bias towards action.  Straight out of the Silicon Valley playbook, this seems incredibly obvious once you hear the rationale, but is certainly not standard practice for folks in their careers today. As the pace of change accelerates all around you, if you're not moving forward, then you are in essence moving backwards.  Simply doing things, when you're guided by your best instincts, generates opportunities, new connections and most of all, helps you learn about yourself. Instead of thinking about whether you want to make a move to another industry, get out there and talk to the folks in that industry or take a small and reversible step in that direction by working in that industry on the side, testing your \\"strong hypothesis, weakly held\\" to see if it is in fact right for you.  To further this focus on action and to help the reader in his or her own career journey, the authors end each chapter with a section titled Invest In Yourself. It provides many eminently useful \\"action items\\" that the reader should take to implement the ideas in the book. Many business and self-help books falter when it comes time to turn ideas into action but these sections are a cut above the tired and obvious action items present in many books.  Overall, this is a hugely refreshing and remarkably coherent career book. Thoroughly researched and backed up by real-world examples, this is a must-read for anyone interested in advancing their career in today's working world.

B0045HCJKS|4.0|Michael Crichton's debut film begins with a great science fiction concept; near-future theme parks with life-like robots programmed to satisfy the customers' every whim and hedonistic desire.  However, at the turning point, when the creators of the theme parks loose control of the life-like androids, Crichton's directing becomes rather campy; he hadn't yet figured out how to effectivly pace and film suspense/horror scenes for maximum effect.  Regardless, the true value of this film lies in the fact that it was a ground breaking prototype for subsequent blockbuster films (e.g., Halloween, Terminator, Logan's Run).  Without question, Yul Brynner as the murderous \\"WesternWorld\\" robot gunslinger was a great choice for the film.  Speaking only a handful of scripted lines, Brynner's brilliance shines through in his physical comportment and his facial expressions (or, mainly, the lack of them); Brynner's methodically paced yet determined pursuit of protagonist Richard Benjamin was very influential in the development of colossal film characters such as \\"Michael Myers\\" and of Schwarzenegger's portrayal of the \\"terminator.\\"  As stated by director John Carpenter,\\"if one goes back and looks at Westworld, that picture involved a robot gunfighter that keeps coming back again and again. I copied a bit of that idea and added it to a horror film on Halloween night with teenagers. To make Michael Myers frightening, I had him walk like a man, not a monster.\\" Also significant to the film was its (at the time) groundbreaking use of \\"digital image processing\\"; pixilated photo imagery that simulated Brynner's android point of view, and his ability to \\"see\\" differences in thermal activity.  This concept seems to have been borrowed and enhanced in the subsequent film PREDATOR where, as in WestWorld, the use of thermal sensory in order to track prey, becomes a primary element.

0307888908|5.0|I've read my fair share of career books. While each takes its own angle on the whole \\"job\\" thing, I find that oftentimes I get most annoyed in the section where authors - typically, at least - say \\"OK now, the first thing you need to do is follow your passion!\\" Luckily, this book takes an entirely different approach.<br /><br />In the start-up of you, Reid Hoffman and Ben Casnocha give a framework for how to think about your career. This framework, obviously, is adapted from the start-up world. The strategies that start-ups use to survive can also be used for individuals to survive (especially given the current state of our economy). And, because of this framework, the question isn't \\"what do you really, with all of your heart, want to do?\\" (which I appreciate, because how many people really have a good answer to that question?) The questions are: what are you good at? What resources do you have at your disposal (could be money, connections, location, etc)? What are the needs in the market? These are the same questions that start-ups must ask as they get going and pivot as needed.<br /><br />I actually used these questions when I myself was looking for a job. Rather than staring at my computer screen thinking \\"hmmmmm maybe I could be a botanist?\\" I made lists of people I knew, their positions and their companies, and worked from there. This helped me map out my network and see what resources I had at my disposal, people-wise.<br /><br />But these aren't just strategies for how to survive, they're strategies for how to thrive. Each chapter contains numerous stories of ultra-successful people from SIlicon Valley and how they used an entrepreneurial attitude to get where they are. And I'll give you a hint: none of them applied to jobs through Craigslist.

B006HGXGWU|5.0|Every year, there are a handful of films that enter and leave theaters very quietly, then pop up out of nowhere to appear all over critics' year-end lists. Take Shelter premiered last year at Sundance and ran the festival circuit, picking up a handful of awards along the way, before opening in limited release back in September. Though it failed to make enough money to recoup its already modest $5 million budget, critics raved about the film and its central performance by Michael Shannon. Shannon plays Curtis LaForche, an Ohio family man who begins dreaming about an apocalyptic storm. Deciding at first to keep the dreams to himself, Curtis pours all of his energy into building a storm shelter in his back yard; his obsession with the shelter eventually strains all the meaningful relationships in his life. A piece of subtle, nuanced filmmaking, I feel Michael Shannon's performance alone made the film worth seeing, but the film itself is a perfect blend of drama and dread. It's a slow-burn psychological mindbender that builds tension gradually, so it's not for everyone, but you can be sure you'll be witnessing some top notch filmmaking if you watch it.

B000WS4PCO|5.0|Suddenly I had, through the courtesy of a gift card, a copy of what is (arguably) U2's most universally acclaimed recording, \\"The Joshua Tree\\", the first time I'd ever owned this release on CD, after at least two versions of the officially released tape had been worn down to ribbons and levitated away in particulate. Although I'd purchased it the day of its release in 1987 and it was nearly always in the dash of my Corolla's cassette player, by the time I'd gotten hold of the CD version I hadn't listened to it in in probably close to ten years. And boy howdy, what a revelation it turned out to be, with a sense of depth and nuance my ears just weren't able to register from listening to it on cassette, bringing to me, at least, a new level of clarity toward what I already regarded as U2's most cohesive release.<br /><br />Raw, churning, and aggressive, with a bristly, expansive, almost infinite guitar sound, \\"Bullet The Blue Sky\\" is the only track from \\"The Joshua Tree\\" that isn't internally consistent with the remainder of the CD, which unites the anthemic, lively vitality of \\"Boy\\", \\"October\\", and \\"War\\" with the experimental ambience of its immediate predecessor, \\"The Unforgettable Fire\\", and the incorporation of stylistic elements from folk, blues, country,  gospel, and a tinge of western Americana to create something heavily textural, timeless and familiar, immediate, and yet rooted in the distinctive harmonics consistent with the band's previous musical trajectory. Sounding out the most universally familiar elements associated with their previous musical arrangements, \\"Where The Streets Have No Name\\" re-introduces us to the heavily resonant elemental bassline Adam Clayton uses to anchor the bottom end, the loping percussive work of Larry Mullen,Jr that merges in tandem with it, and The Edge's delayed, heavily processed, infinitely fragmenting guitar line, but this time it emerges from the soaring ambience of a cathedral-like tone of a synthesizer in a fully-realized, conventionally structured song patiently building over the course of almost the first two minutes into an explosive crescendo before Bono delivers an impassioned plea for a place where there is no sense of separation and demarcation, but universal embracement instead. Indeed, the production values bring a sense of balance and restraint to songs that could just as easily have gone  from the start in the direction of sustained, explosive release or the unwinding martial excitement of their anthemic material; witness the integration of acoustic guitar into tracks such as \\"I Still Haven't Found What I'm Looking For\\" and \\"Running To Stand Still\\" \\"In God's Country\\", or \\"Exit\\", the first two levelly ascending, the third faster in tempo but not at the level of explosive crescendo, and the last, with its added eerie, pulsating atmospherics, reaching sustained explosive release.  The rhythmically insistent  \\"With Or Without You\\" and elegiac \\"One Tree Hill\\" nestle their fluidly progressing guitar lines and percussive flourishes within a rich bed of synthesized ambience.<br /><br />\\"The Joshua Tree\\" has its moments of political commentary, but if \\"Bullet The Blue Sky\\" is removed from the equation, they aren't in the nature of utter polemically-charged jeremiads; \\"Red Hill Mining Town\\" mourns the decline of a community that once was closely knit but coming unraveled through severe economic upheaval; \\"Running To Stand Still\\" explores the negative impact of the drug trade no matter in which marginalized community it's situated within through the eyes of an addict. \\"Mothers Of The Disappeared\\", the sublime closing track, separates the victims of brutal regimes in the Southern Hemisphere into a mutually exclusive, jointly exhaustive category away from their persecutors, their dignity celebrated while assignation of blame is made implicit rather than explicit to keep the focus on those who were removed and the ones who mourn them, the musical arrangement of the track itself builtaround a simple melody plucked out on acoustic guitar,simple bassline, rudimentary percussion, the explosive plash of a looping drone, and a haunting, atmospheric keyboard line that build toward an explosive crescendo, literally ending \\"The Joshua Tree\\" on a high note.<br /><br />Some critics have already revised their opinion with regard to the overall strength and cohesion of \\"The Joshua Tree\\", finding it dated, cliched, and wanting. I'm not someone who agrees with that interpretation. An inevitable backlash resulted as this release catapulted U2 to the status as being possibly not only the most universally recognized band in the world, but also one with a sense of passion, intensity, and conviction that had not been as strongly realized in years. That backlash occurred as the band became infatuated not only with translating the sound of American music for ears outside of North America, but also lecturing Americans on the impact their government's policies were having on the world, and that sense of appropriation coupled with their approbation triggered a long second look at the band. For me this release is a proposition that exists within a level bubble through which none of that intrudes, and I submit to you that it's their finest moment, and one well worth getting.

This isn't a classic haunted house film like Robert Wise's The Haunting or The Shining but its a fun little film with a flaw holding it back from greatness.<br />I mention Radcliffe only in saying that every actor in the film outside of acting talent which they have seems more to have been cast for their faces. He's joined by the excellent Ciaran Hinds playing a local landowner who doesn't believe all this woman in black nonsense despite having a mentally unstable wife played by Janet Mcteer.<br />The Woman in Black herself is a beautiful creation, a ethereal image that plays like English Freddy Krueger, a vengeful spirit who causes the death of children when she's seen in the town of Crythin Gifford, gathering the spirits of her victims around her as sort of a haunting troupe.<br />As I said the real star of the film is James Watkins not to take anything from the work of the cinematographer or production designer. The film does use sound to create more measured bits of suspense included a bit lifted Susan Hill's novel involving a rocking chair behind a locked room and it works well. Then there's the house on Eel Marsh itself is a beautiful creation in keeping with the period setting of the film, a reclusive decaying mansion surrounded by marshes and a tide that cuts it off from town in order to strand Radcliffe.<br />It all adds up to a really good horror film, one with a dark edge to its action and one worth seeing in theatres to get the experience from the sights and sounds created to drive the suspense. There's just one true negative for me and that's the ending of the movie perhaps put in the film for Radcliffe's star power, or perhaps the result of negative testing. B002C5B0T6|5.0|Henry Stuart Hazlitt (1894-1993) was an American economic journalist (e.g., for such publications as The Wall Street Journal, The Nation, Newsweek, and The New York Times). He was also the author of books such as [[ASIN:1933550112 The Failure of the New Economics]], [[ASIN:B000WU2632 Man vs. The Welfare State]], [[ASIN:1172529019 The Inflation Crisis, And How To Resolve It]], [[ASIN:B000WU1IJK Conquest of Poverty, The]], etc.<br /><br />This book was originally published in 1946, and then revised in 1979. He wrote in the original Preface, \\"This book is an analysis of economic fallacies that are at last so prevalent that they have almost become a new orthodoxy... its effort is to show that many of the ideas which now pass for brilliant innovations and advances are in fact mere revivals of ancient errors...\\"<br /><br />The \\"single lesson\\" is stated quite plainly in the first chapter: \\"The art of economics consists in looking not merely at the immediate but at the longer effects of any act or policy; it consists in tracing the consequences of that policy not merely for one group but for all groups.\\" (Pg. 17) He refers to failing to look at the long-run effects of a policy intended to help only a special group the \\"fallacy of overlooking secondary consequences.\\" (Pg. 15-16)<br /><br />Hazlitt is no anarcho-libertarian, and admits that a \\"certain amount of public spending is necessary to perform essential government functions.\\" (Pg. 32) But he is most opposed to public works intended as a means of \\"providing employment.\\" (Pg. 32) If a bridge costs $10 million, he argues that taxpayers have that much taken away from what they would otherwise have spent it on, and \\"for every public job created by the bridge project a private job has been destroyed somewhere else.\\" (Pg. 33)<br /><br />He states that when the government makes loans or subsidies to business, \\"what it does is to tax successful private business in order to support unsuccessful private business.\\" (Pg. 47-48) He argues that it is \\"foolish\\" to try to preserve obsolescent industries or methods of production. (Pg. 102)<br /><br />He opposes government minimum wages, as \\"raising them by government fiat is the wrong way and worse way.\\" The \\"best way,\\" he suggests, is \\"to raise marginal labor productivity\\" (e.g., by greater use of machines, etc.). (Pg. 139)<br /><br />He concedes that \\"There may be times when an increase in debt is a minor consideration as against the gains achieved with the borrowed funds; when a government subsidy is unavoidable to achieve a certain military purpose... But we ought to make sure ... that all the implications of a proposal have been studied. And this is seldom done.\\" (Pg. 193)<br /><br />Hazlitt's book is a provocative and intriguing statement of free market economics, and will be of interest to almost anyone concerned with economic or policy questions.argues that it is \\"foolish\\" to try to preserve obsolescent industries or methods of production. (Pg. 102)<br /><br />He opposes government minimum wages, as \\"raising them by government fiat is the wrong way and worse way.\\" The \\"best way,\\" he suggests, is \\"to raise marginal labor productivity\\" (e.g., by greater use of machines, etc.). (Pg. 139)<br /><br />He concedes that \\"There may be times when an increase in debt is a minor consideration as against the gains achieved with the borrowed funds; when a government subsidy is unavoidable to achieve a certain military purpose... But we ought to make sure ... that all the implications of a proposal have been studied. And this is seldom done.\\" (Pg. 193)<br /><br />Hazlitt's book is a provocative and intriguing statement of free market economics, and will be of interest to almost anyone concerned with economic or policy questions.

0205313426|5.0|The Elements of Style changed the way I approach writing. The book is thin, only about a hundred pages, and you can read it in two or three hours. If you're even remotely interested in making your writing clearer and more effective (and if you're reading this, I'll hazard you are) buy it and read it. Keep it on your shelf; take it down when you need assistance or a refresher.<br /><br />At first, I didn't quite get this book. I kept waiting for Strunk and White to tell me something I didn't know - which happened sooner than I expected, and I realized the grammarians were imparting \\"rules\\" or conventions I knew intuitively, but couldn't explain when or why they ought to be applied (and I've read other grammar books and style guides). The Elements of Style is presented as a series of dictums, not usually my style of learning, but the dictums accumulate and morph into a kind of philosophy. And once you get through a dozen or so rules, you should almost be able to guess what's coming next.<br /><br />Not everything Strunk and White said is still applicable. For example, they argue you shouldn't say `forceful,' but rather `forcible,' but the language has mutated or evolved to where `forceful' is perfectly acceptable and `forcible' is rarely used. However, this book ought to make you see areas where you need improvement. It helped make my writing less wordy and, or so I'd like to think, more impactful. \\"Write in the active, not in the passive,\\" is darned good advice. \\"Put the important words at a sentence's end.\\" Ditto. In all the grammar books I'd read, I'd never read that before. Why use `owing to the fact that' when you can use `since' or `because'? Why say `in the event that' when the word `if' will do?<br /><br />There are other books which claim to expand on Strunk and Whites ideas, but I don't see what you'd need them. You don't need examples for every possible problem. You should be able to use The Elements of Style to logically conclude what you should do in cases not explicitly mentioned. That's part of the beauty of the book: it's larger than the sum of its parts. Well, that and it's just so very right.<br /><br />Troy Parfitt is the author of Why China Will Never Rule the Worldat's part of the beauty of the book: it's larger than the sum of its parts. Well, that and it's just so very right.  Troy Parfitt is the author of Why China Will Never Rule the World

0307959856|2.0|Like many others who have come to love Darcy and Elizabeth,  I am always eager for stories that continue their lives beyond the end of Jane Austen's marvelous romance.  This effort by P. D. James manages to be even more disappointing that others.  James states in her author'a note at the beginning of the book that Miss Austen did not choose to dwell on such odious subjects as murder and had she wished to do so, she would have written the story herself - and done it better.  Indeed, Ms. James fails to capture either the tone of Miss Austen's marvelous prose or her deft characterizations of those involved in the story.  Elizabeth and Darcy come off as confused supporting players rather than the protagonists they are meant to be.  Other characters are given short shrift, especially Jane and Bingley, who seem to appear solely as a sop to the reader's sensibility.  James' one line throw away entries to attempt to link the Elliots of Persuasion and the Knightleys of Emma fall flat and seem to exist solely to alert the reader that James has read Austen and believes herself equipped to blend all the stories into a unified whole.  Most lacking, however, is the detail and leisurely pace that makes the Austen novels such a rich and rewarding experience.  The murder is most awkwardly solved in the final pages of the novel and in a most unsatisfactory fashion.  I admit that creating a continuation of Pride and Prejudice that would do credit to the original is a very daunting task and on that should only be undertaken by the most proficient of writers with a superb understanding of Jane Austen, her characters and the world in which they lived.  This attempt is paltry and bound to disappoint.

The history of all of this blew my mind, from the schools in the 50s sending letters home to parents stating that their child was a \\"problem case\\" if they didn't constantly speak up and socialize in class, to the shift of being quiet, reserved, and shy from personality traits to actual pathologies like social anxiety disorder. This book helped me realize that I am perfectly justified if I prefer to stay in and read a book as opposed to go out to a party, and that being introverted is normal and not something that needs to be \\"fixed.\\"  Every page I read hit home, as if this book was written about me personally.  If you have ever felt alienated for being an introvert, READ THIS BOOK.

1558908846|5.0|Wow...has it been 16 years already since this movie was first released? I remember taking my girlfriend to see this movie back in the summer of 1995 and we both enjoyed it. By then I was already a big fan of the Judge Dredd comic books and knew the character well. I still own my entire collection of all the Eagle Comics Judge Dredd series. Artist Brian Bolland's beautifully illustrated covers were the main draw for me, and the great stories inside hooked me further and kept me along for the ride throughout the series run. When I first heard that Hollywood was making a big-budget live action feature based on the adventures of the famous Judge from Mega City One, I couldn't believe it and anticipatedly awaited it's release. To be honest, I do not think that Sylvester Stallone was the most ideal choice to play Judge Dredd...He's okay in the role I guess. But, I actually thought that Dolph Lundgren would have been a better pick to play Dredd. Lundgren just had the more perfect features and physical attributes such as height to play Dredd. But Stallone had more box-office clout and whether we like it or not, this movie probably would not have gotten the kind of huge financial backing that it did had Stallone not signed on to star in it.<br /><br />The movie takes place in a dystopic future where mankind lives under giant glass-dome-shielded cities fortified by huge surrounding concrete walls that protect it's inhabitants from the lawless mutants and savages who dwell outside the city limits in what is known as the Cursed Earth; it's ravaged wastelands destroyed by past world wars and other conflicts. The cities are overpopulated and under-resourced to the point of chaos and anarchy, and the peace is dutifully maintained by the Judges, street law enforcers who are given total power to be Judge, Jury, and Executioners if needed in their patrols on the streets of Mega City 1.<br /><br />Stallone stars as Judge Dredd, the most feared and respected of all the Judges; a man who holds up the ideals of law and justice to a T and never waivers from the rules of the book. \\"I am the Law!\\", is one of his most famous lines, shouted here triumphantly in the movie by Stallone. A noble ideal that would later be questioned by Dredd himself in following issues of the comic books--but I digress. During a call to help pacify a violent outbreak between hundreds of agitated citizens in an area of Mega City, Judge Dredd winds up killing a bunch of perps and quickly stops the uprising using extreme lethal force which, much like Dirty Harry he is known for. His methods are brutal, but they get results. Meanwhile, a news reporter, who has been running a series of stories based on Dredd's aggressive tactics sometimes used to uphold the law within the city, is suddenly assassinated in his apartment by a person caught on video surveillance dressed up as a street Judge. Evidence at the crime scene strongly points to it being Judge Dredd himself who is the perpetrator of the crime, and so it goes that Dredd is found guilty of the dirty deed and sent to a penal colony to serve out a lifetime sentence among hundreds of other perpetrators that he himself has helped send there in past months. On the way to serve out his sentence, the transport ship taking Dredd and other convicts to the Penal Colony is shot down by the evil Angel Clan, a sadistic rag tag group of cannibalistic family members who have survived for years in the harsh wastelands of the Cursed Earth.<br /><br />Will Judge Dredd be able to survive in the unforgiving Cursed Earth, defeat the Angel Clan and be able to return to Mega City One to bring justice to those who have framed him for murder, and are now causing havoc, mass destruction, and trying to usurp power and control of Mega City 1? You will just have to watch the movie to find that out.<br /><br />Judge Dredd is one of those rare examples of a huge summer blockbuster movie that ended up bombing big-time at the box office, and one which critics mostly hated. But it is still a worthwhile and enjoyable movie to watch even after all of these years, maybe even moreso now than when it was first released. The action is exciting and plentiful, the sets, costume and prop designs, and production values are all top-notch and very impressive. The CGI effects themselves are for the most part pretty laughable, but they still have some redeeming qualities here and there such as the scenes showing the vastness and detail of Mega City one itself. The story is pretty good and involving as well, with enough interesting plot twists and turns to keep your attention. The acting isn't the greatest however...Stallone as Dredd plays it pretty much one-dimensional here, but is still fun to watch as he does what he does best, which is to take the bad guys out with the trash. And Armand Assante hams it up quite a bit as Rico, but for the most part portrays a pretty effective adversary for Dredd. And the rest of the cast for the most part plays it all pretty straight and narrow and nicely done...especially Max Von Sydow as the Chief Judge Fargo, and Diane Lane as Judge Hershey. A young Rob Schneider, fresh from his stint at SNL here, plays the comic interest, and reluctant sidekick to Dredd.<br /><br />Other highlights of the film include the very intimidating behemoth robot known as the ABC Warrior, an out-of-service robotic warrior used in past conflicts that Rico reactivates and now uses as his personal bodyguard, and the character known as Mean Machine Angel, a sadistic part human/part cybernetic cannibal Cursed Earth dweller who along with the rest of the Angel clan wants to kill, cook and eat Judge Dredd.<br /><br />If you are looking for some big-budget Sci-Fi action and adventure to keep you entertained for an hour and a half, give Mega City One's most notorious Judge Dredd a try.hile and enjoyable movie to watch even after all of these years, maybe even moreso now than when it was first released. 1451627289|3.0|The triumphs in this book, as well as the disappointments, all come down to the details.  In parts, the writing is transcendent and thought-provoking.  In parts, the plot is compelling.  However, the details....whether too many or too few...drag the book down.  Other reviewers here have commented on the dragging middle section of the book, and they are completely right.  Tighter editing was needed here.  We are treated to two descriptions of a variety show....and it is the same variety show.  We learn about two school plays in excrutiating detail, involving minor characters.  We see girls jump roping over and over and over.  We slog through endless descriptions of Oswald and his long-suffering wife.  It is not that there are too many details.  It is that all the wrong details are emphasized.  Do we really neeed to know in gruesome detail about both Jake's and Sadie's medical recovery?  No.  Would we actually like to find out what exactly made them fall in love with each other? Yes.  And those details are sadly lacking.  Meanwhile, other details make no sense at all.  Here's are just a couple of examples....Jake keeps placing bets with shady bookies, and the whole time I'm thinking, why not just drive to Vegas and place a bet legitimately? Another detail that particularly bothered me was when Jake says \\"too bad Al didn't think to write down any stocks that would do well\\".  Unless you are living under a rock, you would know to buy IBM and GM and other blue chip stocks in that time period, and things like Apple, Intel, and Microsoft later.  I do not buy that Sadie would finally pry from Jake the fact that he is, indeed, from the future, and then hardly ask any questions about it.  I mean, really?  You wouldn't be a little bit more curious?  It is perhaps odd to complain about realism in a time-travel novel, yet the characters' behavior often does not seem realistic.  There are other more significant problems with this book as well.  To begin with, we are treated to a view of the world that is very skewed to the left.  Well, OK, I can live with that...it is Stephen King after all.  I know what I'm getting into when I pick up a book of his.  In this case, however, it detracts from the book's effect.  For example, having the Iranian hostage crisis become worse when President Reagan takes office....yeah, right.  Comments about Barack Obama also come to mind.  The next problem with this book is the ending.  (Spoiler ahead).  I cannot buy that Jake would undo the Kennedy rescue only to not go find Sadie.  I can't believe that he didn't go to Savannah to woo her and marry her and prevent her from marrying psycho broom man in the first place.  Then, the reader is led to believe that Sadie led such a selfless life helping others because Jake did not interefere and have a relationship with her.  Is this to suggest that she would not have led a life volunteering or helping others if she DID fall and love with him again and marry him?  Plenty of people are married and have families and still volunteer and help others.  I was very put off by this.  The whole ending was anti-climatic and unsatisfactory.  Lastly, the major problem I have with this book is the moral question.  King paints a picture of the world that is almost post-apoctalyptic due to the asassination being thwarted.  The way this is all laid out is very interesting.  However, the thoughts behind that are that 1) one man's action can affect the entire world leading you to believe that 2) there is nothing and no-one in control of the universe.  Maybe there is some random machine or puppet or something, but certainly nothing like, gee, let me think, GOD.  That is a nice thought if you are an atheist; however, if you are not an atheist, the premise is rather troubling (to say the least).  The one moralistic part I do like is King's implication that environment shapes character and behavior.  If you do not delve too deeply, it is an enjoyable book.  It is a shame, however, that it does not hold up to close scrutiny.

But then again, I don't think the author agreed with everything she wrote either.  My impression is that the author researched and compared some of the core principles of American and French parenting, then tried to use the ones that worked best for her.  Which is exactly what I intend to do with the ideas presented here.  This book is not a parenting manual or even a \\"How To\\" book for raising a quiet kid who'll know the difference between camembert and brie at the age of 3.  Instead, it's a set of well written stories about a woman raising her children in a culture that views being a parent differently than America.  It describes how the French believe that parents come first, that having a well behaved child isn't about how the child acts but how the parent acts, and that bringing up a child with good manners and a respect for others is a lot of work.  I'm ready to get to work and start creating Cadre for our little girl.  My only regret is that once I send my child to preschool, she won't be in an environment where 3 year children get tiny 4 course meals planned out by a team of gourmet chefs. B000H309P6|5.0|August '72 Peter Hammill decides to pull the plug on Van Der Graff Generator at the height of it's power opting for a solo career. This album recorded in early '73 reflects the turmoil and struggle of the band and the man. There is an ever present sadness an isolation a paranoia written in the lines that does not relent...slowly leading you into the darkness. Somehow the overall REALIZATION of this work is as relevant as ever. As people were still going through the same development of psyche, IMO. Hammill pours his emotion into these songs, while maintaining a criptic strategy (glasnost) that uncoded reveals a startling truth: \\"Big Brother\\" wants you to program yourself! Remember that while spending time with electronics you've fitted yourself with. There are machines that can record your thoughts and dreams and do! The original album is long 50:40, most of the time I listen to tracks 1,3,5,8 and one of the other songs or bonus tracks. The other four members of VDGG appear sporaticly, most notably on 'Black Room/ The Tower'. This is a VDGG song Hammills' chameleon voice features shades of David Cousins even Klaus Meine. Hammill is a true mimic, the Mocking bird or Brown Thrasher of Rock-n-Roll. yet no one sounds like him. On other albums he sounds like Arthur Brown, Alice Cooper, Roy Harper, Geddy Lee, Lou Reed, Bowie, Robert Calvert, Edgar Broughton, Ric Ocasek, etc. etc. Then his music reminds you of many many more. Yet when you hear \\"Lemmingings/Cog\\" or \\"In The End\\" two completely different songs there's nothing to compare them to. In the 70's alone he released 7 band, 8 solo and 2 double live albums and more. Penned nearly all the songs, recorded, semi-engineered and produced most of his work. He recorded 3 albums from Feb.'73 - Mar.'74 mostly at his home studio. This approach certainly was'nt cutting edge and the results are recordings more akin to late 60's pioneering stereo engineering efforts. I can't think of any comparitive albums to these three, by anyone. The tower is a stunning expose' of audio-visualization like \\"Lighthouse\\" from 'Pawn Hearts'. It did'nt hit me upon first listen, but then...I suggest listening to this song in the dark for maximum effect. It is afterall part of (In The) Black Room. This is music that will appeal to only a small minority. The way I rate albums this is an 8.1 so 5 stars. It's no 'Pawn Hearts' it is the closest to a VDGG album of all his solo work.

078324228X|3.0|The cinematic achievement, The Hurricane, is an inspiring story of Rubin \\"Hurricane\\" Carter who is allegedly convicted for a triple homicide.  His journey through prison, his relationship with a boy bent on freeing him, and his subsequent exoneration all intertwine to create a powerful film that sheds light on police corruption, rampant racism, and innocent men locked behind prison bars.  Although the movie provides an entertaining story, the factual side of the story is wrongly portrayed.  Whether it was a move to allow the movie to become more gripping or whether it was a move of ignorance, the film's direction completely walks the line between reality and illusion.  Viewers who are subject to the film without previous knowledge of Carter's actual story may take what is given in the film as fact.  However, even though a disclaimer warns viewers of the fictionalized story, I believe that the film should've altered the story to make it its own, avoiding its warranted criticism.  If this move had been done, the movie would've been able to still stand on its own legs, all the while giving homage to Rubin Carter.  Fact and fiction aside, the seamless integration of two separate stories works well.  On one hand, Rubin's life in prison and back-story is explored extensively (albeit subjectively).  On the other hand, Lesra Martin, a fifteen-year-old boy, takes an interest in Rubin and progressively works to exonerate him.  Going back to my previous point, the story of Rubin almost seems subjective and forces the viewer to believe what the film would like the viewer to believe.  I found this frustrating as I would prefer to be able to decide on my own what to accept as true and what to accept as false.  The portrayal of the detective, Della Pesca, seemed to be a figurative \\"slap-in-the-face\\".  I feel as though his true character in real life probably wasn't so bent on locking up Rubin -- I may be wrong about this, but the film never gave me a chance to make my own opinion.All in all, The Hurricane is a fantastic piece of cinematic power.  The film packs a punch with an unrelenting glimpse into the racial prejudice common amongst Americans during that time period.  Denzel Washington's portrayal of Rubin Carter was haunting and as Washington pleaded with his lawyers, explaining that he had been locked up for thirty years, I received a chill, almost believing every single word the man said.  Forgetting the factual inaccuracies, this movie was one to be remembered and is certainly a cornerstone for rich storytelling.

0307959856|3.0|I should preface this review by saying that in general I am not a fan of mystery or suspense novels. I'm too impatient, too eager to know how it ends, too comfortable flipping to the last chapter long before I'm supposed to. So in general, I stay away from mysteries. This mystery, however, was billed by many reviewers and interviewers as one of the best Pride & Prejudice sequels out there. I am an avowed Jane Austen fanatic, so of course I had to try it out.<br /><br />As a mystery, I think this was a... satisfying read. On the eve of their biggest ball of the year, Elizabeth and Mr. Darcy (and some guests) are surprised by the arrival of Lizzie's sister Lydia, shouting about gunshots in the woods and her fear that her husband George Wickham is dead. Darcy and Co. go out to investigate and find Wickham crouching over the bloody and definitely deceased body of his friend Denny. What follows is an account of the investigation, the trial of Mr. Wickham, and most interestingly (to me) how Darcy and Elizabeth deal with their part in the whole nasty affair. While reading I was never tempted to flip to the back and find out \\"whodunit\\", but I was always eager to keep reading and see what happened next.<br /><br />The book was a satisfactory sequel to Pride & Prejudice as well, although perhaps not as gush-worthy as some of the reviews I had read. P.D. James did a beautiful job expressing the police and legal systems of the time, her descriptions of the relationships between the various characters were thorough and lovely as well. The one thing I felt was missing from Death Comes to Pemberly as a P&P sequel was the joy and humor so prevalent in Austen's original. However, I recognize that \\"a mystery in the style of Jane Austen\\" may not necessarily need to have the same wit and humor; and that the inclusion of those things may actually detract from the mystery aspect of the story. Still, because I was reading this as an Austen fan and not a mystery fan, I was a little disappointed in the lack.<br /><br />I think if you are a mystery fan or a P.D. James fan you will love this book. If, however, you are an Austen fan, and reading it only for that reason, you should know that this is first and foremost a murder mystery, and temper your expectations accordingly.I think if you are a mystery fan or a P.D. Because if you're just browsing, and start with Wool, then you're going to read Wool 2, and that is where you are going to be lost, because you cannot read Wool 2 without reading 3, 4 and 5.  They're that good.  Wool 2, Proper Gauge, goes into the world that Howey has created and draws you into it with the most fully-realized visualization of a fictional society since Edgar Rice Burroughs gave us Barsoom, but then also gives the reader a sympathetic lead character and the inklings of a darkness in the silo that has nothing to do with the absence of sunlight.  By the time you finish book 2, you will be hooked, and the cliffhanger ending of 3, the Unraveling, will propel you into 4 and 5 before you know what hit you.  So, why buy the Omnibus?  Two reasons:  First, you'll save money, and second, you'll save time.  You'll pay more for each installment separately (5 is $2.99), and when you finish 3, you won't want to wait for 4 or 5.<br /><br />Buy the omnibus.  It's a bargain.

B006ZEDZNY|4.0|To appreciate the quality of this rambling little film that is actually based on an episode in the life of Hunter S. Thompson the following biographical information is helpful: `Hunter Stockton Thompson (1937 - 2005) was an American journalist and author who wrote The Rum Diary (published in1998), Fear and Loathing in Las Vegas (1971), Fear and Loathing on the Campaign Trail '72 (1973), The Curse of Lono (1983), and Hell's Angels: The Strange and Terrible Saga of the Outlaw Motorcycle Gangs (1966). He is credited as the creator of Gonzo journalism, a style of reporting where reporters involve themselves in the action to such a degree that they become central figures of their stories. He is known also for his lifelong use of alcohol, LSD, mescaline, and cocaine (among other substances); his love of firearms; his inveterate hatred of Richard Nixon; and his iconoclastic contempt for authoritarianism. In 1960 Thompson moved to San Juan, Puerto Rico, to take a job with the sporting magazine El Sportivo, which folded soon after his arrival. Thompson applied for a job with the Puerto Rico English-language daily The San Juan Star, but its managing editor, future novelist William J. Kennedy, turned him down. Nonetheless, the two became friends and after the demise of El Sportivo, Thompson worked as a stringer for the New York Herald Tribune and a few stateside papers on Caribbean issues with Kennedy working as his editor. While suffering a bout of health problems, he committed suicide in 2005 at the age of 67.'<br /><br />In the film version of Thompson's autobiographical book, Paul Kemp (Johnny Depp) is a freelance journalist who finds himself at a critical turning point in his life while writing for a run-down newspaper in the Caribbean under toupeed editor Lotterman (Richard Jenkins). He is accompanied by the staff photographer Sala (Michael Rispoli), the drunken staff writer Moberg (Giovanni Ribisi) in his discovery of the many wonders of Puerto Rico, not the least of which is a group of entrepreneurs lead by Sanderson (Aaron Eckhart) - who happens to be living with the stunning Chenault (Amber Heard) who Paul is attracted to - and a series of disreputable business men bent on `touristifyng' a neighboring island.  Paul is a drunk and finds innumerable distractions and challenges as he tries to worm his way through the playing field of lost souls.<br /><br />The story is Hunter S. Thompson all the way and Bruce Robinson has adapted Thompson's book in much the same way the novelist communicates: Robinson also directs. B004OYDVT0|5.0|I am an eclectic  audiophile and purchased this album about a year ago and have listened to its entirety over and over to the point of boredom. I had filed her away, but recently burned a copy for my fiance', who instantly fell in love with Adele...and now I have fallen in love with her all over again. So I pulled out her album again, and am on the second wind of playing her over and over again.  I felt motivated to write a review by some negative reviews.   I heartily disagree with some viewers' comments that every song on this album is whiny.   Quite the contrary, this album runs the gamut of emotions, much like the life-cycle of a relationship: ecstasy, torment, anger, resolve, and acceptance.  This album will be timeless and one of those albums that I would want to have with me if I happen to be stranded on an island. Pure artistry and raw talent. no gimmicks of vocal synthesization via computers- which is refreshing in an age of lip synch and computerized music.
